<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Persian polymath, physician and philosopher (c.980–1037)</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the crater, see <a href="/wiki/Avicenna_(crater)" title="Avicenna (crater)">Avicenna (crater)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Ibn Sīnā" redirects here. Not to be confused with <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Ali_Sina_(disambiguation)" title="Ali Sina (disambiguation)">Ali Sina (activist)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bu-Ali_Sina_University" title="Bu-Ali Sina University">Bu-Ali Sina University</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Lenin_Peak" title="Lenin Peak">Lenin Peak</a>.</div>

<p><b>Ibn Sina</b> (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="fa">ابن سینا</span>; 980 – June 1037 CE), commonly known in the West as <b>Avicenna</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span></span>-/</a></span></span>), was a <a href="/wiki/Persians" title="Persians">Persian</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymath</a> who is regarded as one of the most significant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physicians" title="Physicians">physicians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomers" title="Astronomers">astronomers</a>, philosophers, and writers of the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> and the father of early modern medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sajjad_H._Rizvi" title="Sajjad H. Rizvi">Sajjad H. Rizvi</a> has called Avicenna "arguably the most influential philosopher of the pre-modern era".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> He was a Muslim <a href="/wiki/Peripatetic_school" title="Peripatetic school">Peripatetic</a> philosopher influenced by Greek <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aristotelian_philosophy" title="Aristotelian philosophy">Aristotelian philosophy</a>. Of the 450 works  he is believed to have written, around 240 have survived, including 150 on philosophy and 40 on medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacTutor_Biography|id=Avicenna_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacTutor_Biography|id=Avicenna-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>His most famous works are <i><a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_Healing" title="The Book of Healing">The Book of Healing</a></i>, a philosophical and scientific encyclopedia, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Canon_of_Medicine" title="The Canon of Medicine">The Canon of Medicine</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Medical_encyclopedia" title="Medical encyclopedia">medical encyclopedia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> which became a standard medical text at many medieval <a href="/wiki/University" title="University">universities</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> and remained in use as late as 1650.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Besides philosophy and medicine, Avicenna's corpus includes writings on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomy_in_medieval_Islam" title="Astronomy in medieval Islam">astronomy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alchemy_and_chemistry_in_medieval_Islam" title="Alchemy and chemistry in medieval Islam">alchemy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geography_and_cartography_in_medieval_Islam" title="Geography and cartography in medieval Islam">geography and geology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychology_in_medieval_Islam" title="Psychology in medieval Islam">psychology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_theology" title="Islamic theology">Islamic theology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Logic_in_Islamic_philosophy" title="Logic in Islamic philosophy">logic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematics_in_medieval_Islam" title="Mathematics in medieval Islam">mathematics</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physics_in_medieval_Islam" title="Physics in medieval Islam">physics</a>, and works of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_poetry" title="Islamic poetry">poetry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h2>
<p><i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Avicenna</i></span></i> is a <a href="/wiki/Latinisation_of_names" title="Latinisation of names">Latin corruption</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_name#Nasab" title="Arabic name">Arabic patronym</a> <b>Ibn Sīnā</b> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ابن سينا</span></span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> meaning "Son of Sina". However, Avicenna was not the son but the great-great-grandson of a man named Sina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> His formal <a href="/wiki/Arabic_name" title="Arabic name">Arabic name</a> was <b>Abū ʿAlī al-Ḥusayn bin ʿAbdullāh ibn al-Ḥasan bin ʿAlī bin Sīnā al-Balkhi al-Bukhari</b> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">أبو علي الحسين بن عبد الله بن الحسن بن علي بن سينا البلخي البخاري</span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Circumstances">Circumstances</span></h2>
<p>Avicenna created an extensive corpus of works during what is commonly known as the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a>, in which the translations of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Roman" title="Greco-Roman">Greco-Roman</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indian</a> texts were studied extensively. Greco-Roman (<a href="/wiki/Middle_Platonism" title="Middle Platonism">Mid-</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neo-Platonic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelian</a>) texts translated by the <a href="/wiki/Al-Kindi" title="Al-Kindi">Kindi</a> school were commented, redacted and developed substantially by Islamic intellectuals, who also built upon Persian and <a href="/wiki/Indian_mathematics" title="Indian mathematics">Indian mathematical</a> systems, <a href="/wiki/Indian_astronomy" title="Indian astronomy">astronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algebra" title="Algebra">algebra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trigonometry" title="Trigonometry">trigonometry</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Iranian_Medicine" title="Ancient Iranian Medicine">medicine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samanid_dynasty" title="Samanid dynasty">Samanid dynasty</a> in the eastern part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Greater Khorasan</a> and Central Asia as well as the <a href="/wiki/Buyid_dynasty" title="Buyid dynasty">Buyid dynasty</a> in the western part of Persia and <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> provided a thriving atmosphere for scholarly and cultural development. Under the Samanids, <a href="/wiki/Bukhara" title="Bukhara">Bukhara</a> rivaled <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> as a cultural capital of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_world" title="Islamic world">Islamic world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> There, Avicenna had access to the great libraries of <a href="/wiki/Balkh" title="Balkh">Balkh</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khwarezm" title="Khwarezm">Khwarezm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gorgan" title="Gorgan">Gorgan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ray,_Iran" title="Ray, Iran">Rey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isfahan" title="Isfahan">Isfahan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hamadan" title="Hamadan">Hamadan</a>.
</p><p>Various texts (such as the 'Ahd with Bahmanyar) show that Avicenna debated philosophical points with the greatest scholars of the time. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aruzi_Samarqandi" title="Aruzi Samarqandi">Aruzi Samarqandi</a> describes how before Avicenna left <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khwarezm" title="Khwarezm">Khwarezm</a> he had met <a href="/wiki/Al-Biruni" title="Al-Biruni">Al-Biruni</a> (a famous scientist and astronomer), <a href="/wiki/Abu_Nasr_Mansur" title="Abu Nasr Mansur">Abu Nasr Iraqi</a> (a renowned mathematician), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Sahl_Masihi" title="Abu Sahl Masihi">Abu Sahl Masihi</a> (a respected philosopher) and Abu al-Khayr Khammar (a great physician). The study of the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">Hadith</a> also thrived, and Islamic philosophy, <i><a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">fiqh</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalaam" title="Kalaam">kalaam</a></i>) were all further developed by Avicenna and his opponents at this time.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span></h3>
<p>Avicenna was born in <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 980</span> in the village of Afshana in <a href="/wiki/Transoxiana" title="Transoxiana">Transoxiana</a> to a family of Persian stock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The village was near the <a href="/wiki/Samanid_Empire" title="Samanid Empire">Samanid</a> capital of <a href="/wiki/Bukhara" title="Bukhara">Bukhara</a>, which was his mother's hometown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoichon1986941_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoichon1986941-24">[24]</a></sup> His father Abd Allah was a native of the city of <a href="/wiki/Balkh" title="Balkh">Balkh</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tukharistan" title="Tukharistan">Tukharistan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas201411_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas201411-25">[25]</a></sup> An official of the Samanid bureaucracy, he had served as the governor of a village of the royal estate of Harmaytan (near Bukhara) during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Nuh_II" title="Nuh II">Nuh II</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> 976–997</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas201411_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas201411-25">[25]</a></sup> Avicenna also had a younger brother. A few years later, the family settled in Bukhara, a center of learning, which attracted many scholars. It was there that Avicenna was educated, which early on was seemingly administered by his father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas201412_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas201412-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson20138_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson20138-28">[28]</a></sup> Although both Avicenna's father and brother had converted to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ismailism" title="Ismailism">Ismailism</a>, he himself did not follow the faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaftary2017191_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaftary2017191-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaftary2007202–203_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaftary2007202–203-30">[30]</a></sup> He was instead an adherent of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a> school, which was also followed by the Samanids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas1988330–331_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas1988330–331-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Avicenna was first schooled in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> and literature, and by the age of 10, he had <a href="/wiki/Hafiz_(Quran)" title="Hafiz (Quran)">memorized the entire Quran</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas201412_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas201412-27">[27]</a></sup> He was later sent by his father to an Indian greengrocer, who taught him <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic" title="Arithmetic">arithmetic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas201413_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas201413-32">[32]</a></sup> Afterwards, he was schooled in <a href="/wiki/Jurisprudence" title="Jurisprudence">Jurisprudence</a> by the Hanafi <a href="/wiki/Jurist" title="Jurist">jurist</a> Ismail al-Zahid. Some time later, Avicenna's father invited the physician and philosopher Abu Abdallah al-Natili to their house to educate Avicenna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas201412_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas201412-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson20138_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson20138-28">[28]</a></sup> Together, they studied the <i><a href="/wiki/Isagoge" title="Isagoge">Isagoge</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Porphyry_(philosopher)" title="Porphyry (philosopher)">Porphyry</a> (died 305) and possibly the <a href="/wiki/Categories_(Aristotle)" title="Categories (Aristotle)"><i>Categories</i></a> of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (died 322 BC) as well. After Avicenna had read the <i><a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">Almagest</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> (died 170) and <i><a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements" title="Euclid's Elements">Euclid's Elements</a></i>, Natili told him to continue his research independently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson20138_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson20138-28">[28]</a></sup> By the time Avicenna was eighteen, he was well-educated in Greek sciences. Although Avicenna only mentions Natili as his teacher in his <a href="/wiki/Autobiography" title="Autobiography">autobiography</a>, he most likely had other teachers as well, such as the physicians Abu Mansur Qumri and <a href="/wiki/Abu_Sahl_%27Isa_ibn_Yahya_al-Masihi" title="Abu Sahl 'Isa ibn Yahya al-Masihi">Abu Sahl al-Masihi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas201413_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas201413-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Bukhara_and_Gurganj">In Bukhara and Gurganj</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Transoxiana_8th_century.svg"><img alt="Geophysical map of southern Central Asia (Khurasan and Transoxiana) with the major settlements and regions" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="630" data-file-width="1300" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Transoxiana_8th_century.svg/300px-Transoxiana_8th_century.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Transoxiana_8th_century.svg/450px-Transoxiana_8th_century.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Transoxiana_8th_century.svg/600px-Transoxiana_8th_century.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Transoxiana_8th_century.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khurasan" title="Khurasan">Khurasan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transoxiana" title="Transoxiana">Transoxiana</a></div></div></div>
<p>At the age of seventeen, Avicenna was made a physician of <a href="/wiki/Nuh_II" title="Nuh II">Nuh II</a>. By the time Avicenna was at least 21 years old, his father died. He was subsequently given an administrative post, possibly succeeding his father as the governor of Harmaytan. Avicenna later moved to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gurganj" title="Gurganj">Gurganj</a>, the capital of <a href="/wiki/Khwarazm" title="Khwarazm">Khwarazm</a>, which he reports that he did due to "necessity". The date he went to the place is uncertain, as he reports that he served the <i><a href="/wiki/Khwarazmshah" title="Khwarazmshah">Khwarazmshah</a></i> (ruler) of the region, the <a href="/wiki/Ma%27munids" title="Ma'munids">Ma'munid</a> <a href="/wiki/Abu_al-Hasan_Ali" title="Abu al-Hasan Ali">Abu al-Hasan Ali</a>. The latter ruled from 997 to 1009, which indicates that Avicenna moved sometime during that period. He may have moved in 999, the year which the Samanid state fell after the Turkic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qarakhanids" title="Qarakhanids">Qarakhanids</a> captured Bukhara and imprisoned the Samanid ruler <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Malik_II_(Samanid_emir)" title="Abd al-Malik II (Samanid emir)">Abd al-Malik II</a>. Due to his high position and strong connection with the Samanids, Avicenna may have found himself in an unfavorable position after the fall of his suzerain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70-26">[26]</a></sup> It was through the minister of Gurganj, Abu'l-Husayn as-Sahi, a patron of Greek sciences, that Avicenna entered into the service of Abu al-Hasan Ali.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas201419_(see_also_note_28)_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas201419_(see_also_note_28)-33">[33]</a></sup> Under the Ma'munids, Gurganj became a centre of learning, attracting many prominent figures, such as Avicenna and his former teacher Abu Sahl al-Masihi, the mathematician <a href="/wiki/Abu_Nasr_Mansur" title="Abu Nasr Mansur">Abu Nasr Mansur</a>, the physician <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Khammar" title="Ibn al-Khammar">Ibn al-Khammar</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philologist" title="Philologist">philologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Al-Tha%27alibi" title="Al-Tha'alibi">al-Tha'alibi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBosworth19781066_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBosworth19781066-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBosworth1984a762–764_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBosworth1984a762–764-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Gurgan">In Gurgan</span></h4>
<p>Avicenna later moved due to "necessity" once more (in 1012), this time to the west. There he travelled through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khurasan" title="Khurasan">Khurasani</a> cities of <a href="/wiki/Nisa,_Turkmenistan" title="Nisa, Turkmenistan">Nasa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abivard" title="Abivard">Abivard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tus,_Iran" title="Tus, Iran">Tus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samangan,_Torbat-e_Jam" title="Samangan, Torbat-e Jam">Samangan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jajarm" title="Jajarm">Jajarm</a>. He was planning to visit the ruler of the city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gurgan" title="Gurgan">Gurgan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ziyarid_dynasty" title="Ziyarid dynasty">Ziyarid</a> <a href="/wiki/Qabus" title="Qabus">Qabus</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> 977–981, 997–1012</span>), a cultivated patron of writing, whose court attracted many distinguished poets and scholars. However, when Avicenna eventually arrived, he discovered that the ruler had been dead since the winter of 1013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMadelung1975215_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMadelung1975215-36">[36]</a></sup> Avicenna then left Gurgan for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dihistan" title="Dihistan">Dihistan</a>, but returned after becoming ill. There he met <a href="/wiki/Abu_%27Ubayd_al-Juzjani" title="Abu 'Ubayd al-Juzjani">Abu 'Ubayd al-Juzjani</a> (died 1070) who became his pupil and companion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas201419,_29_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas201419,_29-37">[37]</a></sup> Avicenna stayed briefly in Gurgan, reportedly serving Qabus' son and successor <a href="/wiki/Manuchihr" title="Manuchihr">Manuchihr</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> 1012–1031</span>) and resided in the house of a patron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70_26-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Ray_and_Hamadan">In Ray and Hamadan</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gold_dinar_of_Majd_al-Dawla,_the_last_ruler_of_the_Buyid_amirate_of_Ray.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="392" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Gold_dinar_of_Majd_al-Dawla%2C_the_last_ruler_of_the_Buyid_amirate_of_Ray.jpg/220px-Gold_dinar_of_Majd_al-Dawla%2C_the_last_ruler_of_the_Buyid_amirate_of_Ray.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Gold_dinar_of_Majd_al-Dawla%2C_the_last_ruler_of_the_Buyid_amirate_of_Ray.jpg/330px-Gold_dinar_of_Majd_al-Dawla%2C_the_last_ruler_of_the_Buyid_amirate_of_Ray.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Gold_dinar_of_Majd_al-Dawla%2C_the_last_ruler_of_the_Buyid_amirate_of_Ray.jpg/440px-Gold_dinar_of_Majd_al-Dawla%2C_the_last_ruler_of_the_Buyid_amirate_of_Ray.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gold_dinar_of_Majd_al-Dawla,_the_last_ruler_of_the_Buyid_amirate_of_Ray.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coin of <a href="/wiki/Majd_al-Dawla" title="Majd al-Dawla">Majd al-Dawla</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> 997–1029</span>), the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amir" title="Amir">amir</a></i> (ruler) of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buyid" title="Buyid">Buyid</a> branch of <a href="/wiki/Ray,_Iran" title="Ray, Iran">Ray</a></div></div></div>
<p>In <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1014</span>, Avicenna went to the city of <a href="/wiki/Ray,_Iran" title="Ray, Iran">Ray</a>, where he entered into the service of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buyid" title="Buyid">Buyid</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amir" title="Amir">amir</a></i> (ruler) <a href="/wiki/Majd_al-Dawla" title="Majd al-Dawla">Majd al-Dawla</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> 997–1029</span>) and his mother <a href="/wiki/Sayyida_Shirin" title="Sayyida Shirin">Sayyida Shirin</a>, the <i>de facto</i> ruler of the realm. There he served as the physician at the court, treating Majd al-Dawla, who was suffering from <a href="/wiki/Melancholia" title="Melancholia">melancholia</a>. Avicenna reportedly later served as the "business manager" of Sayyida Shirin in <a href="/wiki/Qazvin" title="Qazvin">Qazvin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hamadan" title="Hamadan">Hamadan</a>, though details regarding this tenure are unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70_26-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson201314_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson201314-38">[38]</a></sup> During this period, Avicenna finished his <i><a href="/wiki/The_Canon_of_Medicine" title="The Canon of Medicine">Canon of Medicine</a></i>, and started writing his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Book_of_Healing" title="Book of Healing">Book of Healing</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson201314_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson201314-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1015, during Avicenna's stay in Hamadan, he participated in a public debate, as was custom for newly arrived scholars in western Iran at that time. The purpose of the debate was to examine one's reputation against a prominent local resident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson201315–16_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson201315–16-39">[39]</a></sup> The person whom Avicenna debated against was Abu'l-Qasim al-Kirmani, a member of the school of philosophers of <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson201315_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson201315-40">[40]</a></sup> The debate became heated, resulting in Avicenna accusing Abu'l-Qasim of lack of basic knowledge in <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, while Abu'l-Qasim accused Avicenna of impoliteness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson201315–16_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson201315–16-39">[39]</a></sup> After the debate, Avicenna sent a letter to the Baghdad Peripatetics, asking if Abu'l-Qasim's claim that he shared the same opinion as them was true. Abu'l-Qasim later retaliated by writing a letter to an unknown person, in which he made accusations so serious, that Avicenna wrote to a deputy of Majd al-Dawla, named Abu Sa'd, to investigate the matter. The accusation made towards Avicenna may have been the same as he had received earlier, in which he was accused by the people of Hamadan of copying the stylistic structures of the Quran in his <i>Sermons on Divine Unity</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson201316–18_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson201316–18-41">[41]</a></sup> The seriousness of this charge, in the words of the historian Peter Adamson, "cannot be underestimated in the larger Muslim culture."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson201317_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson201317-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Not long afterwards, Avicenna shifted his allegiance to the rising Buyid <i>amir</i> <a href="/wiki/Shams_al-Dawla" title="Shams al-Dawla">Shams al-Dawla</a> (the younger brother of Majd al-Dawla), which Adamson suggests was due to Abu'l-Qasim also working under Sayyida Shirin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson201318_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson201318-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMadelung1975293_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMadelung1975293-44">[44]</a></sup> Avicenna had been called upon by Shams al-Dawla to treat him, but after the latters campaign in the same year against his former ally, the <a href="/wiki/Annazids" title="Annazids">Annazid</a> ruler Abu Shawk (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> 1010–1046</span>), he forced Avicenna to become his <a href="/wiki/Vizier" title="Vizier">vizier</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson201318_(see_also_note_45)_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson201318_(see_also_note_45)-45">[45]</a></sup> Although Avicenna would sometimes clash with Shams al-Dawla's troops, he remained vizier until the latter died of <a href="/wiki/Colic" title="Colic">colic</a> in 1021. Avicenna was asked by Shams al-Dawla's son and successor <a href="/wiki/Sama%27_al-Dawla" title="Sama' al-Dawla">Sama' al-Dawla</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> 1021–1023</span>) to stay as vizier, but instead went into hiding with his patron Abu Ghalib al-Attar, to wait for better opportunities to emerge. It was during this period that Avicenna was secretly in contact with <a href="/wiki/Ala_al-Dawla_Muhammad" title="Ala al-Dawla Muhammad">Ala al-Dawla Muhammad</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> 1008–1041</span>), the <a href="/wiki/Kakuyids" title="Kakuyids">Kakuyid</a> ruler of <a href="/wiki/Isfahan" title="Isfahan">Isfahan</a> and uncle of Sayyida Shirin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70_26-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson201322_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson201322-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBosworth1984b773–774_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBosworth1984b773–774-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>It was during his stay at Attar's home that Avicenna completed his <i>Book of Healing</i>, writing 50 pages a day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson201322–23_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson201322–23-48">[48]</a></sup> The Buyid court in Hamadan, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">Kurdish</a> vizier Taj al-Mulk, suspected Avicenna of correspondence with Ala al-Dawla, and as result had the house of Attar ransacked and Avicenna imprisoned in the fortress of Fardajan, outside Hamadan. Juzjani blames one of Avicenna's informers for his capture. Avicenna was imprisoned for four months, until Ala al-Dawla captured Hamadan, thus putting an end to Sama al-Dawla's reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70_26-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson201323_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson201323-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Isfahan">In Isfahan</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MuhammadibnRustamDushmanziyarCoin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="412" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/MuhammadibnRustamDushmanziyarCoin.jpg/220px-MuhammadibnRustamDushmanziyarCoin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/MuhammadibnRustamDushmanziyarCoin.jpg/330px-MuhammadibnRustamDushmanziyarCoin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/MuhammadibnRustamDushmanziyarCoin.jpg/440px-MuhammadibnRustamDushmanziyarCoin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MuhammadibnRustamDushmanziyarCoin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coin of <a href="/wiki/Ala_al-Dawla_Muhammad" title="Ala al-Dawla Muhammad">Ala al-Dawla Muhammad</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> 1008–1041</span>), the <a href="/wiki/Kakuyids" title="Kakuyids">Kakuyid</a> ruler of <a href="/wiki/Isfahan" title="Isfahan">Isfahan</a></div></div></div>
<p>Avicenna was subsequently released, and went to Isfahan, where he was well received by Ala al-Dawla. In the words of Juzjani, the Kakuyid ruler gave Avicenna "the respect and esteem which someone like him deserved."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70_26-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70-26">[26]</a></sup> Adamson also says that Avicenna's service under Ala al-Dawla "proved to be the most stable period of his life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson201325_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson201325-50">[50]</a></sup> Avicenna served as the advisor, if not vizier of Ala al-Dawla, accompanying him in many of his military expeditions and travels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70_26-10"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas198767–70-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson201325_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson201325-50">[50]</a></sup> Avicenna dedicated two Persian works to him, a philosophical treatise named <i><a href="/wiki/Daneshnameh-ye_Alai" title="Daneshnameh-ye Alai">Danish-nama-yi Ala'i</a></i> ("Book of Science for Ala"), and a medical treatise about the pulse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELazard1975630_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELazard1975630-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9C%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%B9_%D0%90%D0%B2%D0%B8%D1%86%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BD%D1%8B_1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/%D0%9C%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%B9_%D0%90%D0%B2%D0%B8%D1%86%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BD%D1%8B_1.JPG/220px-%D0%9C%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%B9_%D0%90%D0%B2%D0%B8%D1%86%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BD%D1%8B_1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/%D0%9C%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%B9_%D0%90%D0%B2%D0%B8%D1%86%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BD%D1%8B_1.JPG/330px-%D0%9C%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%B9_%D0%90%D0%B2%D0%B8%D1%86%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BD%D1%8B_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/%D0%9C%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%B9_%D0%90%D0%B2%D0%B8%D1%86%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BD%D1%8B_1.JPG/440px-%D0%9C%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%B9_%D0%90%D0%B2%D0%B8%D1%86%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BD%D1%8B_1.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9C%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%B9_%D0%90%D0%B2%D0%B8%D1%86%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BD%D1%8B_1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Avicenna_Mausoleum" title="Avicenna Mausoleum">Mausoleum of Avicenna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamadan" title="Hamadan">Hamadan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a></div></div></div>
<p>During the brief occupation of Isfahan by the <a href="/wiki/Ghaznavids" title="Ghaznavids">Ghaznavids</a> in January 1030, Avicenna and Ala al-Dawla relocated to the southwestern Iranian region of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khuzestan_Province" title="Khuzestan Province">Khuzistan</a>, where they stayed until the death of the Ghaznavid ruler <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_of_Ghazni" title="Mahmud of Ghazni">Mahmud</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> 998–1030</span>), which occurred two months later. It was seemingly when Avicenna returned to Isfahan that he started writing his <i>Pointers and Reminders</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGutas2014133_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGutas2014133-52">[52]</a></sup> In 1037, while Avicenna was accompanying Ala al-Dawla to a battle near Isfahan, he was hit by a severe colic, which he had been constantly suffering from throughout his life. He died shortly afterwards in Hamadan, where he was buried.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson201326_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson201326-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span></h2>
<p>Avicenna wrote extensively on <a href="/wiki/Early_Islamic_philosophy" title="Early Islamic philosophy">early Islamic philosophy</a>, especially the subjects <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>, including treatises named <i>Logic</i> and <i>Metaphysics</i>. Most of his works were written in <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>—then the language of science in the Middle East—and some in <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>. Of linguistic significance even to this day are a few books that he wrote in nearly pure Persian language (particularly the Danishnamah-yi 'Ala', Philosophy for Ala' ad-Dawla'). Avicenna's commentaries on Aristotle often criticized the philosopher,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> encouraging a lively debate in the spirit of <a href="/wiki/Ijtihad" title="Ijtihad">ijtihad</a>.
</p><p>Avicenna's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platonism_in_Islamic_Philosophy" title="Platonism in Islamic Philosophy">Neoplatonic</a> scheme of "emanations" became fundamental in the <i><a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">Kalam</a></i> (school of theological discourse) in the 12th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>His <i>Book of Healing</i> became available in Europe in partial Latin translation some fifty years after its composition, under the title <i>Sufficientia</i>, and some authors have identified a "Latin Avicennism" as flourishing for some time, paralleling the more influential Latin <a href="/wiki/Averroism" title="Averroism">Averroism</a>, but suppressed by the <a href="/wiki/Condemnations_of_1210%E2%80%931277" title="Condemnations of 1210–1277">Parisian decrees of 1210 and 1215</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Avicenna's psychology and theory of knowledge influenced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_of_Auvergne,_Bishop_of_Paris" title="William of Auvergne, Bishop of Paris">William of Auvergne, Bishop of Paris</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEP_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEP-56">[56]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Albertus_Magnus" title="Albertus Magnus">Albertus Magnus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEP_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-IEP-56">[56]</a></sup> while his metaphysics influenced the thought of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEP_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-IEP-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metaphysical_doctrine">Metaphysical doctrine</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px 0;overflow:hidden;width:238px;border-collapse:collapse;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em}.mw-parser-output .ambox-speedy{border-left:10px solid #b32424;background-color:#fee7e6}.mw-parser-output .ambox-delete{border-left:10px solid #b32424}.mw-parser-output .ambox-content{border-left:10px solid #f28500}.mw-parser-output .ambox-style{border-left:10px solid #fc3}.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em
