0;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-empty-cell{border:none;padding:0;width:1px}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-image-div{width:52px}html.client-js body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Technical plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-technical" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>may be too technical for most readers to understand</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Avicenna&amp;action=edit">help improve it</a> to <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Make_technical_articles_understandable" title="Wikipedia:Make technical articles understandable">make it understandable to non-experts</a>, without removing the technical details.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">January 2014</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Early Islamic philosophy and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_metaphysics" title="Islamic metaphysics">Islamic metaphysics</a>, imbued as it is with <a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">Islamic theology</a>, distinguishes more clearly than Aristotelianism between essence and existence. Whereas existence is the domain of the contingent and the accidental, essence endures within a being beyond the accidental. The philosophy of Avicenna, particularly that part relating to metaphysics, owes much to al-Farabi. The search for a definitive Islamic philosophy separate from <a href="/wiki/Occasionalism" title="Occasionalism">Occasionalism</a> can be seen in what is left of his work.
</p><p>Following al-Farabi's lead, Avicenna initiated a full-fledged inquiry into the question of being, in which he distinguished between essence (<i>Mahiat</i>) and existence (<i>Wujud</i>). He argued that the fact of existence cannot be inferred from or accounted for by the essence of existing things, and that form and matter by themselves cannot interact and originate the movement of the universe or the progressive actualization of existing things. Existence must, therefore, be due to an <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">agent-cause</a> that necessitates, imparts, gives, or adds existence to an essence. To do so, the cause must be an existing thing and coexist with its effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Islam_in_Britannica_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Islam_in_Britannica-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Avicenna's consideration of the essence-attributes question may be elucidated in terms of his ontological analysis of the modalities of being; namely impossibility, contingency and necessity. Avicenna argued that the impossible being is that which cannot exist, while the contingent in itself (<i>mumkin bi-dhatihi</i>) has the potentiality to be or not to be without entailing a contradiction. When actualized, the contingent becomes a 'necessary existent due to what is other than itself' (<i>wajib al-wujud bi-ghayrihi</i>). Thus, contingency-in-itself is potential beingness that could eventually be actualized by an external cause other than itself. The metaphysical structures of necessity and contingency are different. Necessary being due to itself (<i>wajib al-wujud bi-dhatihi</i>) is true in itself, while the contingent being is 'false in itself' and 'true due to something else other than itself'. The necessary is the source of its own being without borrowed existence. It is what always exists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Necessary exists 'due-to-Its-Self', and has no quiddity/essence (<i>mahiyya</i>) other than existence (<i>wujud</i>). Furthermore, It is 'One' (<i>wahid ahad</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> since there cannot be more than one 'Necessary-Existent-due-to-Itself' without differentia (fasl) to distinguish them from each other. Yet, to require differentia entails that they exist 'due-to-themselves' as well as 'due to what is other than themselves'; and this is contradictory. However, if no differentia distinguishes them from each other, then there is no sense in which these 'Existents' are not one and the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nader_El-Bizri_2000_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nader_El-Bizri_2000-61">[61]</a></sup> Avicenna adds that the 'Necessary-Existent-due-to-Itself' has no genus (<i>jins</i>), nor a definition (<i>hadd</i>), nor a counterpart (<i>nadd</i>), nor an opposite (<i>did</i>), and is detached (<i>bari</i>) from matter (<i>madda</i>), quality (<i>kayf</i>), quantity (<i>kam</i>), place (<i>ayn</i>), situation (<i>wad</i>) and time (<i>waqt</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Avicenna's theology on metaphysical issues (<i>ilāhiyyāt</i>) has been criticized by some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_scholars" title="Islamic scholars">Islamic scholars</a>, among them <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">al-Ghazali</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Taymiyya" title="Ibn Taymiyya">Ibn Taymiyya</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Qayyim" title="Ibn al-Qayyim">Ibn al-Qayyim</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (April 2016)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> While discussing the views of the theists among the Greek philosophers, namely <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> in <i>Al-Munqidh min ad-Dalal</i> ("Deliverance from Error"), al-Ghazali noted that the Greek philosophers "must be taxed with unbelief, as must their partisans among the Muslim philosophers, such as Avicenna and al-Farabi and their likes." He added that "None, however, of the Muslim philosophers engaged so much in transmitting Aristotle's lore as did the two men just mentioned. [...] The sum of what we regard as the authentic philosophy of Aristotle, as transmitted by al-Farabi and Avicenna, can be reduced to three parts: a part which must be branded as unbelief; a part which must be stigmatized as innovation; and a part which need not be repudiated at all."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Argument_for_God.27s_existence"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Argument_for_God's_existence">Argument for God's existence</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Proof_of_the_Truthful" title="Proof of the Truthful">Proof of the Truthful</a></div>
<p>Avicenna made an <a href="/wiki/Argument" title="Argument">argument</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">existence of God</a> which would be known as the "<a href="/wiki/Proof_of_the_Truthful" title="Proof of the Truthful">Proof of the Truthful</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>: <i>burhan al-siddiqin</i>). Avicenna argued that there must be a "necessary existent" (Arabic: <i>wajib al-wujud</i>), an entity that cannot <i>not</i> exist<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson2013170_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson2013170-67">[67]</a></sup> and through a series of arguments, he identified it with <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">the Islamic conception of God</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson2013171_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson2013171-68">[68]</a></sup> Present-day <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_philosophy" title="History of philosophy">historian of philosophy</a> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Adamson_(philosopher)" title="Peter Adamson (philosopher)">Peter Adamson</a> called this argument one of the most influential medieval arguments for God's existence, and Avicenna's biggest contribution to the history of philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson2013170_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson2013170-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Al-Biruni_correspondence">Al-Biruni correspondence</span></h3>
<p>Correspondence between Avicenna (with his student Ahmad ibn 'Ali al-Ma'sumi) and <a href="/wiki/Al-Biruni" title="Al-Biruni">Al-Biruni</a> has survived in which they debated <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelian</a> <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Peripatetic_school" title="Peripatetic school">Peripatetic school</a>. Abu Rayhan began by asking Avicenna eighteen questions, ten of which were criticisms of Aristotle's <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Heavens" title="On the Heavens">On the Heavens</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology">Theology</span></h3>
<p>Avicenna was a devout Muslim and sought to reconcile rational philosophy with Islamic theology. His aim was to prove the existence of God and His creation of the world scientifically and through <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a> and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodman-8-9_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goodman-8-9-70">[70]</a></sup> Avicenna's views on Islamic theology (and philosophy) were enormously influential, forming part of the core of the curriculum at Islamic religious schools until the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Avicenna wrote a number of short treatises dealing with Islamic theology. These included treatises on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_prophet" title="Islamic prophet">prophets</a> (whom he viewed as "inspired philosophers"), and also on various scientific and philosophical interpretations of the Quran, such as how Quranic <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a> corresponds to his own philosophical system. In general these treatises linked his philosophical writings to Islamic religious ideas; for example, the body's afterlife.
</p><p>There are occasional brief hints and allusions in his longer works, however, that Avicenna considered philosophy as the only sensible way to distinguish real prophecy from illusion. He did not state this more clearly because of the political implications of such a theory, if prophecy could be questioned, and also because most of the time he was writing shorter works which concentrated on explaining his theories on philosophy and theology clearly, without digressing to consider <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epistemological" title="Epistemological">epistemological</a> matters which could only be properly considered by other philosophers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Later interpretations of Avicenna's philosophy split into three different schools; those (such as <a href="/wiki/Nasir_al-Din_al-Tusi" title="Nasir al-Din al-Tusi">al-Tusi</a>) who continued to apply his philosophy as a system to interpret later political events and scientific advances; those (such as <a href="/wiki/Fakhr_al-Din_al-Razi" title="Fakhr al-Din al-Razi">al-Razi</a>) who considered Avicenna's theological works in isolation from his wider philosophical concerns; and those (such as <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">al-Ghazali</a>) who selectively used parts of his philosophy to support their own attempts to gain greater spiritual insights through a variety of mystical means. It was the theological interpretation championed by those such as al-Razi which eventually came to predominate in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madrasahs" title="Madrasahs">madrasahs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Avicenna <a href="/wiki/Hafiz_(Quran)" title="Hafiz (Quran)">memorized the Quran</a> by the age of ten, and as an adult, he wrote five treatises commenting on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sura" title="Sura">suras</a> from the Quran. One of these texts included the <i>Proof of Prophecies</i>, in which he comments on several Quranic verses and holds the Quran in high esteem. Avicenna argued that the Islamic prophets should be considered higher than philosophers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Avicenna is generally understood to have been aligned with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a> school of thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aisha_Khan_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aisha_Khan-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janssens91_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Janssens91-76">[76]</a></sup> Avicenna studied <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a> law, many of his notable teachers were <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a> jurists, and he served under the Hanafi court of Ali ibn Mamun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aisha_Khan_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aisha_Khan-75">[75]</a></sup> Avicenna said at an early age that he remained "unconvinced" by Ismaili missionary attempts to convert him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aisha_Khan_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-Aisha_Khan-75">[75]</a></sup> Medieval historian Ẓahīr al-dīn al-Bayhaqī (d. 1169) also believed Avicenna to be a follower of the <a href="/wiki/Brethren_of_Purity" title="Brethren of Purity">Brethren of Purity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janssens91_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Janssens91-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thought_experiments">Thought experiments</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Floating_man" title="Floating man">Floating man</a></div>
<p>While he was imprisoned in the castle of Fardajan near Hamadhan, Avicenna wrote his famous "<a href="/wiki/Floating_man" title="Floating man">floating man</a>"—literally falling man—a <a href="/wiki/Thought_experiment" title="Thought experiment">thought experiment</a> to demonstrate human <a href="/wiki/Self-awareness" title="Self-awareness">self-awareness</a> and the substantiality and immateriality of the soul. Avicenna believed his "Floating Man" thought experiment demonstrated that the soul is a substance, and claimed humans cannot doubt their own consciousness, even in a situation that prevents all sensory data input. The thought experiment told its readers to imagine themselves created all at once while suspended in the air, isolated from all <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sensation" title="wikt:sensation">sensations</a>, which includes no sensory contact with even their own bodies. He argued that, in this scenario, one would still have <a href="/wiki/Self-consciousness" title="Self-consciousness">self-consciousness</a>. Because it is conceivable that a person, suspended in air while cut off from <a href="/wiki/Empirical_evidence" title="Empirical evidence">sense experience</a>, would still be capable of determining his own existence, the thought experiment points to the conclusions that the soul is a perfection, independent of the body, and an immaterial substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The conceivability of this "Floating Man" indicates that the soul is perceived intellectually, which entails the soul's separateness from the body. Avicenna referred to the living human <a href="/wiki/Nous" title="Nous">intelligence</a>, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Active_intellect" title="Active intellect">active intellect</a>, which he believed to be the <a href="/wiki/Hypostatic_abstraction" title="Hypostatic abstraction">hypostasis</a> by which God communicates <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> to the human mind and imparts order and intelligibility to nature. Following is an English translation of the argument:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>One of us (i.e. a human being) should be imagined as having been created in a single stroke; created perfect and complete but with his vision obscured so that he cannot perceive external entities; created falling through air or a void, in such a manner that he is not struck by the firmness of the air in any way that compels him to feel it, and with his limbs separated so that they do not come in contact with or touch each other. Then contemplate the following: can he be assured of the existence of himself? He does not have any doubt in that his self exists, without thereby asserting that he has any exterior limbs, nor any internal organs, neither heart nor brain, nor any one of the exterior things at all; but rather he can affirm the existence of himself, without thereby asserting there that this self has any extension in space. Even if it were possible for him in that state to imagine a hand or any other limb, he would not imagine it as being a part of his self, nor as a condition for the existence of that self; for as you know that which is asserted is different from that which is not asserted and that which is inferred is different from that which is not inferred. Therefore the self, the existence of which has been asserted, is a unique characteristic, in as much that it is not as such the same as the body or the limbs, which have not been ascertained. Thus that which is ascertained (i.e. the self), does have a way of being sure of the existence of the soul as something other than the body, even something non-bodily; this he knows, this he should understand intuitively, if it is that he is ignorant of it and needs to be beaten with a stick [to realize it].</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Ibn Sina, Kitab Al-Shifa, On the Soul<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nader_El-Bizri_2000_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nader_El-Bizri_2000-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>However, Avicenna posited the brain as the place where reason interacts with sensation. Sensation prepares the soul to receive rational concepts from the universal Agent Intellect. The first knowledge of the flying person would be "I am," affirming his or her essence. That essence could not be the body, obviously, as the flying person has no sensation. Thus, the knowledge that "I am" is the core of a human being: the soul exists and is self-aware.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Avicenna thus concluded that the idea of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self_(philosophy)" title="Self (philosophy)">self</a> is not logically dependent on any physical <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object (philosophy)">thing</a>, and that the soul should not be seen in <a href="/wiki/Relative_term" title="Relative term">relative terms</a>, but as a primary given, a <a href="/wiki/Substance_theory" title="Substance theory">substance</a>. The body is unnecessary; in relation to it, the soul is its perfection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nader_El-Bizri_2000_pp._149-171_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nader_El-Bizri_2000_pp._149-171-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elbizri67-89_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-elbizri67-89-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> In itself, the soul is an immaterial substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Principal_works">Principal works</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Canon_of_Medicine"><i>The Canon of Medicine</i></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Canon_of_Medicine" title="The Canon of Medicine">The Canon of Medicine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Canons_of_medicine.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="890" data-file-width="1272" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Canons_of_medicine.JPG/220px-Canons_of_medicine.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Canons_of_medicine.JPG/330px-Canons_of_medicine.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Canons_of_medicine.JPG/440px-Canons_of_medicine.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Canons_of_medicine.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Canons of medicine book from Avicenna, Latin translation located at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_Health_Science_Center_at_San_Antonio" title="University of Texas Health Science Center at San Antonio">UT Health of San Antonio</a></div></div></div>
<p>Avicenna authored a five-volume medical encyclopedia: <i>The Canon of Medicine</i> (<i>Al-Qanun fi't-Tibb</i>). It was used as the standard medical textbook in the Islamic world and Europe up to the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacb1_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-jacb1-86">[86]</a></sup> The <i>Canon</i> still plays an important role in <a href="/wiki/Unani_medicine" title="Unani medicine">Unani medicine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liber_Primus_Naturalium"><i>Liber Primus Naturalium</i></span></h3>
<p>Avicenna considered whether events like rare diseases or disorders have natural causes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> He used the example of <a href="/wiki/Polydactyly" title="Polydactyly">polydactyly</a> to explain his perception that causal reasons exist for all medical events. This view of medical phenomena anticipated developments in the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> by seven centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Book_of_Healing"><i>The Book of Healing</i></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_Healing" title="The Book of Healing">The Book of Healing</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Summarize plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section should include only a <strong>brief</strong> summary of <a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_Healing" title="The Book of Healing">The Book of Healing</a>.</b><span class="hide-when-compact"> See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Summary_style" title="Wikipedia:Summary style">Wikipedia:Summary style</a> for information on how to properly incorporate it into this article's main text.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">July 2016</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_sciences">Earth sciences</span></h4>
<p>Avicenna wrote on <a href="/wiki/Earth_science" title="Earth science">Earth sciences</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a> in <i>The Book of Healing</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodfield_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goodfield-90">[90]</a></sup> While discussing the formation of <a href="/wiki/Mountain" title="Mountain">mountains</a>, he explained:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Either they are the effects of upheavals of the crust of the earth, such as might occur during a violent earthquake, or they are the effect of water, which, cutting itself a new route, has denuded the valleys, the strata being of different kinds, some soft, some hard ... It would require a long period of time for all such changes to be accomplished, during which the mountains themselves might be somewhat diminished in size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodfield_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goodfield-90">[90]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy_of_science">Philosophy of science</span></h4>
<p>In the <i>Al-Burhan</i> (<i>On Demonstration</i>) section of <i>The Book of Healing</i>, Avicenna discussed the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_science" title="Philosophy of science">philosophy of science</a> and described an early <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a> of <a href="/wiki/Inquiry" title="Inquiry">inquiry</a>. He discussed Aristotle's <i><a href="/wiki/Posterior_Analytics" title="Posterior Analytics">Posterior Analytics</a></i> and significantly diverged from it on several points. Avicenna discussed the issue of a proper methodology for scientific inquiry and the question of "How does one acquire the first principles of a science?" He asked how a scientist would arrive at "the initial <a href="/wiki/Axiom" title="Axiom">axioms</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">hypotheses</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">deductive</a> science without inferring them from some more basic premises?" He explained that the ideal situation is when one grasps that a "relation holds between the terms, which would allow for absolute, universal certainty". Avicenna then added two further methods for arriving at the <a href="/wiki/First_principle" title="First principle">first principles</a>: the ancient Aristotelian method of <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">induction</a> (<i>istiqra</i>), and the method of <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">examination</a> and <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experimentation</a> (<i>tajriba</i>). Avicenna criticized Aristotelian induction, arguing that "it does not lead to the absolute, universal, and certain premises that it purports to provide." In its place, he developed a "method of experimentation as a means for scientific inquiry."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Logic">Logic</span></h4>
<p>An early formal system of <a href="/wiki/Temporal_logic" title="Temporal logic">temporal logic</a> was studied by Avicenna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Although he did not develop a real theory of temporal propositions, he did study the relationship between <i>temporalis</i> and the implication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Avicenna's work was further developed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Najm_al-D%C4%ABn_al-Qazw%C4%ABn%C4%AB_al-K%C4%81tib%C4%AB" title="Najm al-Dīn al-Qazwīnī al-Kātibī">Najm al-Dīn al-Qazwīnī al-Kātibī</a> and became the dominant system of <a href="/wiki/Logic_in_Islamic_philosophy" title="Logic in Islamic philosophy">Islamic logic</a> until modern times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Avicennian logic also influenced several early European logicians such as <a href="/wiki/Albertus_Magnus" title="Albertus Magnus">Albertus Magnus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/William_of_Ockham" title="William of Ockham">William of Ockham</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Avicenna endorsed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Law_of_non-contradiction" title="Law of non-contradiction">law of non-contradiction</a> proposed by Aristotle, that a fact could not be both true and false at the same time and in the same sense of the terminology used. He stated, "Anyone who denies the law of non-contradiction should be beaten and burned until he admits that to be beaten is not the same as not to be beaten, and to be burned is not the same as not to be burned."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Physics">Physics</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Mechanics" title="Mechanics">mechanics</a>, Avicenna, in <i>The Book of Healing</i>, developed a theory of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motion_(physics)" title="Motion (physics)">motion</a>, in which he made a distinction between the inclination (tendency to motion) and <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">force</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Projectile" title="Projectile">projectile</a>, and concluded that motion was a result of an inclination (<i>mayl</i>) transferred to the projectile by the thrower, and that <a href="/wiki/Projectile_motion" title="Projectile motion">projectile motion</a> in a vacuum would not cease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Espinoza_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Espinoza-100">[100]</a></sup> He viewed inclination as a permanent force whose effect is dissipated by external forces such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_resistance" title="Air resistance">air resistance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>The theory of motion presented by Avicenna was probably influenced by the 6th-century Alexandrian scholar <a href="/wiki/John_Philoponus" title="John Philoponus">John Philoponus</a>. Avicenna's is a less sophisticated variant of the <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_impetus" title="Theory of impetus">theory of impetus</a> developed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buridan" title="Buridan">Buridan</a> in the 14th century. It is unclear if Buridan was influenced by Avicenna, or by Philoponus directly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a>, Avicenna was among those who argued that light had a speed, observing that "if the perception of <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> is due to the emission of some sort of <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">particles</a> by a luminous source, the speed of light must be finite."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> He also provided a wrong explanation of the <a href="/wiki/Rainbow" title="Rainbow">rainbow</a> phenomenon. <a href="/wiki/Carl_Benjamin_Boyer" title="Carl Benjamin Boyer">Carl Benjamin Boyer</a> described Avicenna's ("Ibn Sīnā") theory on the rainbow as follows:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Independent observation had demonstrated to him that the bow is not formed in the dark cloud but rather in the very thin mist lying between the cloud and the sun or observer. The cloud, he thought, serves simply as the background of this thin substance, much as a quicksilver lining is placed upon the rear surface of the glass in a mirror. Ibn Sīnā would change the place not only of the bow, but also of the color formation, holding the iridescence to be merely a subjective sensation in the eye.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In 1253, a Latin text entitled <i>Speculum Tripartitum</i> stated the following regarding Avicenna's theory on <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heat</a>:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Avicenna says in his book of heaven and earth, that heat is generated from motion in external things.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychology">Psychology</span></h4>
<p>Avicenna's legacy in classical psychology is primarily embodied in the <i>Kitab al-nafs</i> parts of his <i>Kitab al-shifa</i> (<i>The Book of Healing</i>) and <i>Kitab al-najat</i> (<i>The Book of Deliverance</i>). These were known in Latin under the title <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_Anima" title="De Anima">De Anima</a> (treatises "on the soul").<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (October 2012)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> – <a href="/wiki/Talk:Avicenna#Dubious" title="Talk:Avicenna">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> Notably, Avicenna develops what is called the <a href="/wiki/Floating_man" title="Floating man">Flying Man</a> argument in the Psychology of <i>The Cure</i> I.1.7 as defence of the argument that the soul is without quantitative extension, which has an affinity with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descartes" title="Descartes">Descartes</a>'s <i>cogito</i> argument (or what <a href="/wiki/Phenomenology_(philosophy)" title="Phenomenology (philosophy)">phenomenology</a> designates as a form of an "<i>epoche</i>").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nader_El-Bizri_2000_pp._149-171_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nader_El-Bizri_2000_pp._149-171-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elbizri67-89_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-elbizri67-89-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Avicenna's psychology requires that connection between the body and soul be strong enough to ensure the soul's individuation, but weak enough to allow for its immortality. Avicenna grounds his psychology on physiology, which means his account of the soul is one that deals almost entirely with the natural science of the body and its abilities of perception. Thus, the philosopher's connection between the soul and body is explained almost entirely by his understanding of perception; in this way, bodily perception interrelates with the immaterial human intellect. In sense perception, the perceiver senses the form of the object; first, by perceiving features of the object by our external senses. This sensory information is supplied to the internal senses, which merge all the pieces into a whole, unified conscious experience. This process of perception and abstraction is the nexus of the soul and body, for the material body may only perceive material objects, while the immaterial soul may only receive the immaterial, universal forms. The way the soul and body interact in the final abstraction of the universal from the concrete particular is the key to their relationship and interaction, which takes place in the physical body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>The soul completes the action of intellection by accepting forms that have been abstracted from matter. This process requires a concrete particular (material) to be abstracted into the universal intelligible (immaterial). The material and immaterial interact through the Active Intellect, which is a "divine light" containing the intelligible forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> The Active Intellect reveals the universals concealed in material objects much like the sun makes colour available to our eyes.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_contributions">Other contributions</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy_and_astrology">Astronomy and astrology</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skulls_of_Avicenna.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="377" data-file-width="514" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Skulls_of_Avicenna.jpg/220px-Skulls_of_Avicenna.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Skulls_of_Avicenna.jpg/330px-Skulls_of_Avicenna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Skulls_of_Avicenna.jpg/440px-Skulls_of_Avicenna.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skulls_of_Avicenna.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skull of Avicenna, found in 1950 during construction of <a href="/wiki/Avicenna_Mausoleum" title="Avicenna Mausoleum">the new mausoleum</a></div></div></div>
<p>Avicenna wrote an attack on astrology titled <i>Resāla fī ebṭāl aḥkām al-nojūm</i>, in which he cited passages from the Quran to dispute the power of astrology to foretell the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> He believed that each planet had some influence on the earth, but argued against astrologers being able to determine the exact effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Avicenna's astronomical writings had some influence on later writers, although in general his work could be considered less developed than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alhazen" title="Alhazen">Alhazen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Al-Biruni" title="Al-Biruni">Al-Biruni</a>. One important feature of his writing is that he considers mathematical astronomy as a separate discipline to astrology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ragep_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ragep-110">[110]</a></sup> He criticized Aristotle's view of the <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> receiving their light from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, stating that the stars are self-luminous, and believed that the <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> are also self-luminous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> He claimed to have observed <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus" title="Transit of Venus">Venus as a spot on the Sun</a>. This is possible, as there was a transit on 24 May 1032, but Avicenna did not give the date of his observation, and modern scholars have questioned whether he could have observed the transit from his location at that time; he may have mistaken a sunspot for Venus. He used his transit observation to help establish that Venus was, at least sometimes, below the Sun in Ptolemaic cosmology,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ragep_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ragep-110">[110]</a></sup> i.e. the sphere of Venus comes before the sphere of the Sun when moving out from the Earth in the prevailing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geocentric" title="Geocentric">geocentric</a> model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldstein_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldstein-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>He also wrote the <i>Summary of the Almagest</i>, (based on <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">Almagest</a></i>), with an appended treatise "to bring that which is stated in the Almagest and what is understood from Natural Science into conformity". For example, Avicenna considers the motion of the solar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apogee" title="Apogee">apogee</a>, which Ptolemy had taken to be fixed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ragep_110-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ragep-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span></h3>
<p>Avicenna was first to derive the attar of flowers from distillation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> and used <a href="/wiki/Steam_distillation" title="Steam distillation">steam distillation</a> to produce essential oils such as rose essence, which he used as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aromatherapeutic" title="Aromatherapeutic">aromatherapeutic</a> treatments for heart conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marlene_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marlene-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike al-Razi, Avicenna explicitly disputed the theory of the <a href="/wiki/Philosopher%27s_stone" title="Philosopher's stone">transmutation of substances</a> commonly believed by <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemists</a>:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Those of the chemical craft know well that no change can be effected in the different species of substances, though they can produce the appearance of such change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Four works on alchemy attributed to Avicenna were translated into <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> as:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anawati_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anawati-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Liber Aboali Abincine de Anima in arte Alchemiae</i></span></li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Declaratio Lapis physici Avicennae filio sui Aboali</i></span></li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Avicennae de congelatione et conglutinatione lapidum</i></span></li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Avicennae ad Hasan Regem epistola de Re recta</i></span></li></ul>
<p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Liber Aboali Abincine de Anima in arte Alchemiae</i></span> was the most influential, having influenced later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval" title="Medieval">medieval</a> chemists and alchemists such as <a href="/wiki/Vincent_of_Beauvais" title="Vincent of Beauvais">Vincent of Beauvais</a>. However, Anawati argues (following Ruska) that the de Anima is a fake by a Spanish author. Similarly the Declaratio is believed not to be actually by Avicenna. The third work (<i>The Book of Minerals</i>) is agreed to be Avicenna's writing, adapted from the <i>Kitab al-Shifa</i> (<i>Book of the Remedy</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anawati_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-Anawati-118">[118]</a></sup> Avicenna classified minerals into stones, fusible substances, sulfurs and salts, building on the ideas of Aristotle and Jabir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> The <i>epistola de Re recta</i> is somewhat less sceptical of alchemy; Anawati argues that it is by Avicenna, but written earlier in his career when he had not yet firmly decided that transmutation was impossible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anawati_118-2"><a href="#cite_note-Anawati-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poetry">Poetry</span></h3>
<p>Almost half of Avicenna's works are versified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> His poems appear in both Arabic and Persian. As an example, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Granville_Browne" title="Edward Granville Browne">Edward Granville Browne</a> claims that the following Persian verses are incorrectly attributed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omar_Khayy%C3%A1m" title="Omar Khayyám">Omar Khayyám</a>, and were originally written by Ibn Sīnā:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1109925960">.mw-parser-output .verse_translation .translated{padding-left:2em}@media only screen and (max-width:43.75em){.mw-parser-output .verse_translation.wrap_when_small td{display:block;padding-left:0}.mw-parser-output .verse_translation.wrap_when_small .translated{padding-left:0}}</style>
<table class="verse_translation wrap_when_small" role="presentation" style="margin-left:1em;">
<tbody><tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td><div class="poem" lang="" style="font-style:roman;text-align:right">
<p><span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">از قعر گل سیاه تا اوج زحل<br/>
کردم همه مشکلات گیتی را حل<br/>
بیرون جستم زقید هر مکر و حیل<br/>
هر بند گشاده شد مگر بند اجل</span></span>
</p>
</div>
</td>
<td class="translated"><div class="poem">
<p>From the depth of the black earth up to Saturn's apogee,<br/>
All the problems of the universe have been solved by me.<br/>
I have escaped from the coils of snares and deceits;<br/>
I have unraveled all knots except the knot of Death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 91">: 91 </span></sup>
</p>
</div>
</td></tr>
<tr style="display: none;">
<td>
</td>
<td style="padding-left: 2em;">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_Islamic_civilization">Classical Islamic civilization</span></h3>
<p>Robert Wisnovsky, a scholar of Avicenna attached to <a href="/wiki/McGill_University" title="McGill University">McGill University</a>, says that "Avicenna was the central figure in the long history of the rational sciences in Islam, particularly in the fields of metaphysics, logic and medicine" but that his works didn't only have an influence in these "secular" fields of knowledge alone, as "these works, or portions of them, were read, taught, copied, commented upon, quoted, paraphrased and cited by thousands of post-Avicennian scholars—not only philosophers, logicians, physicians and specialists in the mathematical or exact sciences, but also by those who specialized in the disciplines of <a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">ʿilm al-kalām</a> (rational theology, but understood to include <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">philosophy of mind</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Islamic_jurisprudence" title="Principles of Islamic jurisprudence">usūl al-fiqh</a> (jurisprudence, but understood to include philosophy of law, dialectic, and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_language" title="Philosophy of language">philosophy of language</a>)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages_and_Renaissance">Middle Ages and Renaissance</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Avicenna_Mausoleum_interior.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="681" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Avicenna_Mausoleum_interior.jpg/170px-Avicenna_Mausoleum_interior.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Avicenna_Mausoleum_interior.jpg/255px-Avicenna_Mausoleum_interior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Avicenna_Mausoleum_interior.jpg/340px-Avicenna_Mausoleum_interior.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Avicenna_Mausoleum_interior.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Inside view of the Avicenna Mausoleum, designed by <a href="/wiki/Hooshang_Seyhoun" title="Hooshang Seyhoun">Hooshang Seyhoun</a> in 1945–1950</div></div></div>
<p>As early as the 14th century when <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante Alighieri</a> depicted him in Limbo alongside the virtuous non-Christian thinkers in his <i><a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy" title="Divine Comedy">Divine Comedy</a></i> such as <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horace" title="Horace">Horace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lucan" title="Lucan">Lucan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saladin" title="Saladin">Saladin</a>. Avicenna has been recognized by both East and West as one of the great figures in intellectual history.  <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> cites Avicenna's opinion when discussing the causes of planetary motions in Chapter 2 of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomia_Nova" title="Astronomia Nova">Astronomia Nova</a></i>.
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kepler_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kepler-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/George_Sarton" title="George Sarton">George Sarton</a>, the author of <i>The History of Science</i>, described Avicenna as "one of the greatest thinkers and medical scholars in history"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zahoor_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zahoor-125">[125]</a></sup> and called him "the most famous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_science" title="Islamic science">scientist of Islam</a> and one of the most famous of all races, places, and times". He was one of the Islamic world's leading writers in the field of medicine.
</p><p>Along with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Zakariya_al-Razi" title="Muhammad ibn Zakariya al-Razi">Rhazes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_al-Qasim_al-Zahrawi" title="Abu al-Qasim al-Zahrawi">Abulcasis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Nafis" title="Ibn al-Nafis">Ibn al-Nafis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hunayn_ibn_Ishaq" title="Hunayn ibn Ishaq">al-Ibadi</a>, Avicenna is considered an important compiler of early Muslim medicine. He is remembered in the Western <a href="/wiki/History_of_medicine" title="History of medicine">history of medicine</a> as a major historical figure who made important contributions to medicine and the European <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>. His medical texts were unusual in that where controversy existed between Galen and Aristotle's views on medical matters (such as anatomy), he preferred to side with Aristotle, where necessary updating Aristotle's position to take into account post-Aristotelian advances in anatomical knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Aristotle's dominant intellectual influence among medieval European scholars meant that Avicenna's linking of Galen's medical writings with Aristotle's philosophical writings in the <i>Canon of Medicine</i> (along with its comprehensive and logical organisation of knowledge) significantly increased Avicenna's importance in medieval Europe in comparison to other Islamic writers on medicine. His influence following translation of the <i>Canon</i> was such that from the early fourteenth to the mid-sixteenth centuries he was ranked with Hippocrates and Galen as one of the acknowledged authorities, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">princeps medicorum</i></span> ("prince of physicians").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_reception">Modern reception</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Monument_Avicenna_in_Qakh.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2248" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Monument_Avicenna_in_Qakh.JPG/220px-Monument_Avicenna_in_Qakh.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Monument_Avicenna_in_Qakh.JPG/330px-Monument_Avicenna_in_Qakh.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Monument_Avicenna_in_Qakh.JPG/440px-Monument_Avicenna_in_Qakh.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Monument_Avicenna_in_Qakh.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A monument to Avicenna in <a href="/wiki/Qakh_(city)" title="Qakh (city)">Qakh (city)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a></div></div></div>
<p>In present-day Iran, Afghanistan and <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, he is considered a national icon, and is often regarded as among the greatest Persians. A monument was erected outside the Bukhara museum.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="Need the year this event took place (February 2015)">year needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avicenna_Mausoleum_and_Museum" title="Avicenna Mausoleum and Museum">Avicenna Mausoleum and Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hamadan" title="Hamadan">Hamadan</a> was built in 1952. <a href="/wiki/Bu-Ali_Sina_University" title="Bu-Ali Sina University">Bu-Ali Sina University</a> in Hamadan (Iran), the biotechnology <a href="/wiki/Avicenna_Research_Institute" title="Avicenna Research Institute">Avicenna Research Institute</a> in Tehran (Iran), the <i>ibn Sīnā</i> Tajik State Medical University in <a href="/wiki/Dushanbe" title="Dushanbe">Dushanbe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Sina_Academy_of_Medieval_Medicine_and_Sciences" title="Ibn Sina Academy of Medieval Medicine and Sciences">Ibn Sina Academy of Medieval Medicine and Sciences</a> at <a href="/wiki/Aligarh" title="Aligarh">Aligarh</a>, India, Avicenna School in <a href="/wiki/Karachi" title="Karachi">Karachi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Avicenna_Medical_College" title="Avicenna Medical College">Avicenna Medical College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a>, Pakistan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Ibn Sina Balkh Medical School in his native province of <a href="/wiki/Balkh" title="Balkh">Balkh</a> in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, Ibni Sina Faculty Of Medicine of Ankara University <a href="/wiki/Ankara" title="Ankara">Ankara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, the main classroom building (the Avicenna Building) of the <a href="/wiki/Sharif_University_of_Technology" title="Sharif University of Technology">Sharif University of Technology</a>, and Ibn Sina Integrated School in Marawi City (Philippines) are all named in his honour. His portrait hangs in the Hall of the Avicenna Faculty of Medicine in the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">University of Paris</a>. There is a crater on the Moon named <a href="/wiki/Avicenna_(crater)" title="Avicenna (crater)">Avicenna</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Mangrove" title="Mangrove">mangrove</a> genus.
</p><p>In 1980, the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, which then ruled his birthplace Bukhara, celebrated the thousandth anniversary of Avicenna's birth by circulating various <a href="/wiki/Commemorative_stamp" title="Commemorative stamp">commemorative stamps</a> with artistic illustrations, and by erecting a <a href="/wiki/Bust_(sculpture)" title="Bust (sculpture)">bust</a> of Avicenna based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthropological" title="Anthropological">anthropological</a> research by Soviet scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Near his birthplace in Qishlak Afshona, some 25 km (16 mi) north of Bukhara, a training college for medical staff has been named for him.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="Need the year this event took place (February 2015)">year needed</span></a></i>]</sup> On the grounds is a museum dedicated to his life, times and work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kennard_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kennard-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Self-published_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on a self-published source. (February 2020)">self-published source?</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TajikistanP17-20Somoni-1999(2000)-donatedsb_f.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="255" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="94" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/TajikistanP17-20Somoni-1999%282000%29-donatedsb_f.jpg/220px-TajikistanP17-20Somoni-1999%282000%29-donatedsb_f.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/TajikistanP17-20Somoni-1999%282000%29-donatedsb_f.jpg/330px-TajikistanP17-20Somoni-1999%282000%29-donatedsb_f.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/TajikistanP17-20Somoni-1999%282000%29-donatedsb_f.jpg/440px-TajikistanP17-20Somoni-1999%282000%29-donatedsb_f.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TajikistanP17-20Somoni-1999(2000)-donatedsb_f.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Image of Avicenna on the <a href="/wiki/Tajikistani_somoni" title="Tajikistani somoni">Tajikistani somoni</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Avicenna_Prize" title="Avicenna Prize">Avicenna Prize</a>, established in 2003, is awarded every two years by <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> and rewards individuals and groups for their achievements in the field of ethics in science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> The aim of the award is to promote ethical reflection on issues raised by advances in science and technology, and to raise global awareness of the importance of ethics in science.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Persian_Scholar_pavilion_in_Viena_UN_(Avicenna).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="2448" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Persian_Scholar_pavilion_in_Viena_UN_%28Avicenna%29.jpg/220px-Persian_Scholar_pavilion_in_Viena_UN_%28Avicenna%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Persian_Scholar_pavilion_in_Viena_UN_%28Avicenna%29.jpg/330px-Persian_Scholar_pavilion_in_Viena_UN_%28Avicenna%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Persian_Scholar_pavilion_in_Viena_UN_%28Avicenna%29.jpg/440px-Persian_Scholar_pavilion_in_Viena_UN_%28Avicenna%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Persian_Scholar_pavilion_in_Viena_UN_(Avicenna).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The statue of Avicenna in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_Office_in_Vienna" title="United Nations Office in Vienna">United Nations Office in Vienna</a> as a part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_Scholars_Pavilion" title="Persian Scholars Pavilion">Persian Scholars Pavilion</a> donated by Iran</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Avicenna_Directories" title="Avicenna Directories">Avicenna Directories</a> (2008–15; now the <a href="/wiki/World_Directory_of_Medical_Schools" title="World Directory of Medical Schools">World Directory of Medical Schools</a>) list universities and schools where doctors, public health practitioners, pharmacists and others, are educated. The original project team stated "Why Avicenna? Avicenna ... was ... noted for his synthesis of knowledge from both east and west. He has had a lasting influence on the development of medicine and health sciences. The use of Avicenna's name symbolises the worldwide partnership that is needed for the promotion of health services of high quality."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 2009, Iran donated a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_Scholars_Pavilion" title="Persian Scholars Pavilion">Persian Scholars Pavilion</a>" to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_Office_in_Vienna" title="United Nations Office in Vienna">United Nations Office in Vienna</a> which is placed in the central <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Memorial_Plaza_(Vienna)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Memorial Plaza (Vienna) (page does not exist)">Memorial Plaza</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vienna_International_Center" title="Vienna International Center">Vienna International Center</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> The "Persian Scholars Pavilion" at United Nations in <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, Austria is featuring the statues of four prominent Iranian figures. Highlighting the Iranian architectural features, the pavilion is adorned with Persian art forms and includes the statues of renowned Iranian scientists Avicenna, <a href="/wiki/Al-Biruni" title="Al-Biruni">Al-Biruni</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Zakariya_al-Razi" title="Muhammad ibn Zakariya al-Razi">Zakariya Razi</a> (Rhazes) and <a href="/wiki/Omar_Khayyam" title="Omar Khayyam">Omar Khayyam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1982 Soviet film <i>Youth of Genius</i> (Russian: <span lang="ru">Юность гения</span>, <small>romanized: </small><span title="Russian-language romanization"><i lang="ru-Latn">Yunost geniya</i></span>) by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Elyor_Ishmukhamedov&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Elyor Ishmukhamedov (page does not exist)">Elyor Ishmukhamedov</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%98%D1%88%D0%BC%D1%83%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B5%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%B2,_%D0%AD%D0%BB%D1%8C%D1%91%D1%80_%D0%9C%D1%83%D1%85%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%B4%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B8%D1%87" title="ru:Ишмухамедов, Эльёр Мухитдинович">ru</a>]</span> recounts Avicenna's younger years. The film is set in Bukhara at the turn of the millennium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Louis_L%27Amour" title="Louis L'Amour">Louis L'Amour</a>'s 1985 historical novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Walking_Drum" title="The Walking Drum">The Walking Drum</a></i>, Kerbouchard studies and discusses Avicenna's <i>The Canon of Medicine</i>.
</p><p>In his book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Physician" title="The Physician">The Physician</a></i> (1988) <a href="/wiki/Noah_Gordon_(novelist)" title="Noah Gordon (novelist)">Noah Gordon</a> tells the story of a young English medical apprentice who disguises himself as a Jew to travel from England to Persia and learn from Avicenna, the great master of his time. The novel was adapted into a feature film, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Physician_(2013_film)" title="The Physician (2013 film)">The Physician</a></i>, in 2013. Avicenna was played by <a href="/wiki/Ben_Kingsley" title="Ben Kingsley">Ben Kingsley</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_works">List of works</span></h2>
<p>The treatises of Avicenna influenced later Muslim thinkers in many areas including theology, philology, mathematics, astronomy, physics and music. His works numbered almost 450 volumes on a wide range of subjects, of which around 240 have survived. In particular, 150 volumes of his surviving works concentrate on philosophy and 40 of them concentrate on medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacTutor_Biography|id=Avicenna_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacTutor_Biography|id=Avicenna-10">[10]</a></sup>
His most famous works are <i>The Book of Healing</i>, and <i>The Canon of Medicine</i>.
</p><p>Avicenna wrote at least one treatise on alchemy, but several others have been falsely attributed to him. His <i>Logic</i>, <i>Metaphysics</i>, <i>Physics</i>, and <i>De Caelo</i>, are treatises giving a synoptic view of <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelian doctrine</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB1911_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-137">[137]</a></sup> though <i>Metaphysics</i> demonstrates a significant departure from the brand of <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonism</a> known as Aristotelianism in Avicenna's world; Arabic philosophers<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (February 2015)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="Need the year this event took place (February 2015)">year needed</span></a></i>]</sup> have hinted at the idea that Avicenna was attempting to "re-Aristotelianise" Muslim philosophy in its entirety, unlike his predecessors, who accepted the conflation of Platonic, Aristotelian, Neo- and Middle-Platonic works transmitted into the Muslim world.
</p><p>The <i>Logic</i> and <i>Metaphysics</i> have been extensively reprinted, the latter, e.g., at Venice in 1493, 1495 and 1546. Some of his shorter essays on medicine, logic, etc., take a poetical form (the poem on logic was published by Schmoelders in 1836).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Two encyclopedic treatises, dealing with philosophy, are often mentioned. The larger, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_Healing" title="The Book of Healing">Al-Shifa'</a></i> (<i>Sanatio</i>), exists nearly complete in manuscript in the <a href="/wiki/Bodleian_Library" title="Bodleian Library">Bodleian Library</a> and elsewhere; part of it on the <i>De Anima</i> appeared at Pavia (1490) as the <i>Liber Sextus Naturalium</i>, and the long account of Avicenna's philosophy given by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Shahrastani" title="Muhammad al-Shahrastani">Muhammad al-Shahrastani</a> seems to be mainly an analysis, and in many places a reproduction, of the Al-Shifa'. A shorter form of the work is known as the An-najat (<i>Liberatio</i>). The Latin editions of part of these works have been modified by the corrections which the monastic editors confess that they applied. There is also a <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">حكمت مشرقيه</span></span> (<i>hikmat-al-mashriqqiyya</i>, in Latin <i>Philosophia Orientalis</i>), mentioned by <a href="/wiki/Roger_Bacon" title="Roger Bacon">Roger Bacon</a>, the majority of which is lost in antiquity, which according to Averroes was pantheistic in tone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB1911_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Avicenna's works further include:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Works_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Works-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>Sirat al-shaykh al-ra'is</i> (<i>The Life of Avicenna</i>), ed. and trans. WE. Gohlman, Albany, NY: State University of New York Press, 1974. (The only critical edition of Avicenna's autobiography, supplemented with material from a biography by his student Abu 'Ubayd al-Juzjani. A more recent translation of the Autobiography appears in D. Gutas, <i>Avicenna and the Aristotelian Tradition: Introduction to Reading Avicenna's Philosophical Works</i>, Leiden: Brill, 1988; second edition 2014.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Works_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-Works-139">[139]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-isharat_wa_al-tanbihat" title="Al-isharat wa al-tanbihat">Al-isharat wa al-tanbihat</a></i> (<i>Remarks and Admonitions</i>), ed. S. Dunya, Cairo, 1960; parts translated by S.C. Inati, Remarks and Admonitions, Part One: Logic, Toronto, Ont.: Pontifical Institute for Mediaeval Studies, 1984, and Ibn Sina and Mysticism, Remarks and Admonitions: Part 4, London: Kegan Paul International, 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Works_139-2"><a href="#cite_note-Works-139">[139]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Al-Qanun fi'l-tibb</i> (<i>The Canon of Medicine</i>), ed. I. a-Qashsh, Cairo, 1987. (Encyclopedia of medicine.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Works_139-3"><a href="#cite_note-Works-139">[139]</a></sup> manuscript,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Latin translation, Flores Avicenne,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Michael de Capella, 1508,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> Modern text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Ahmed Shawkat Al-Shatti, Jibran Jabbur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Risalah fi sirr al-qadar</i> (<i>Essay on the Secret of Destiny</i>), trans. G. Hourani in Reason and Tradition in Islamic Ethics, Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Works_139-4"><a href="#cite_note-Works-139">[139]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Daneshnameh-ye_Alai" title="Daneshnameh-ye Alai">Danishnama-i 'ala'i</a></i> (<i>The Book of Scientific Knowledge</i>), ed. and trans. P. Morewedge, The Metaphysics of Avicenna, London: Routledge and Kegan Paul, 1973.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Works_139-5"><a href="#cite_note-Works-139">[139]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Kitab al-Shifa'</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_Healing" title="The Book of Healing">The Book of Healing</a></i>). (Avicenna's major work on philosophy. He probably began to compose al-Shifa' in 1014, and completed it in 1020.) Critical editions of the Arabic text have been published in Cairo, 1952–83, originally under the supervision of I. Madkour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Works_139-6"><a href="#cite_note-Works-139">[139]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Kitab al-Najat</i> (<i>The Book of Salvation</i>), trans. F. Rahman, <i>Avicenna's Psychology: An English Translation of Kitab al-Najat, Book II, Chapter VI with Historical-philosophical Notes and Textual Improvements on the Cairo Edition</i>, Oxford: Oxford University Press, 1952. (The psychology of al-Shifa'.) (Digital version of the Arabic text)</li>
<li><i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Risala_fi%27l-Ishq&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Risala fi'l-Ishq (page does not exist)">Risala fi'l-Ishq</a></i> (<i><a class="external text" href="http://www.muslimphilosophy.com/sina/works/avicenna-love.pdf" rel="nofollow">A Treatise on Love</a></i>). Translated by Emil L. Fackenheim.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Persian_works">Persian works</span></h3>
<p>Avicenna's most important <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> work is the <i><a href="/wiki/Daneshnameh-ye_Alai" title="Daneshnameh-ye Alai">Danishnama-i 'Alai</a></i> (<span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">دانشنامه علائی</span></span>, "the Book of Knowledge for [Prince] 'Ala ad-Daulah"). Avicenna created new scientific vocabulary that had not previously existed in Persian. The Danishnama covers such topics as logic, metaphysics, music theory and other sciences of his time. It has been translated into English by Parwiz Morewedge in 1977.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> The book is also important in respect to Persian scientific works.
</p><p><i>Andar Danesh-e Rag</i> (<span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">اندر دانش رگ</span></span>, "On the Science of the Pulse") contains nine chapters on the science of the pulse and is a condensed synopsis.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_poetry" title="Persian poetry">Persian poetry</a> from Avicenna is recorded in various manuscripts and later anthologies such as <i><a href="/wiki/Nozhat_al-Majales" title="Nozhat al-Majales">Nozhat al-Majales</a></i>.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Ramin_Jahanbegloo" title="Ramin Jahanbegloo">Ramin Jahanbegloo</a>, <i>In Search of the Sacred : A Conversation with Seyyed Hossein Nasr on His Life and Thought</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABC-CLIO" title="ABC-CLIO">ABC-CLIO</a> (2010), p. 59</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFCorbin2016">Corbin 2016</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://press.princeton.edu/titles/2761.html" rel="nofollow">Overview</a>. "In this work a distinguished scholar of Islamic religion examines the mysticism and psychological thought of the great eleventh-century Persian philosopher and physician Avicenna (Ibn Sina), author of over a hundred works on theology, logic, medicine, and mathematics."</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFPasnauDyke2010">Pasnau &amp; Dyke 2010</a>, p. 52. "Most important of these initially was the massive Book of Healing (Al-Shifa) of the eleventh-century Persian Avicenna, the parts of which labeled in Latin as De anima and De generatione having been translated in the second half of the twelfth century."</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFDaly2013">Daly 2013</a>, p. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=9aZPAQAAQBAJ&amp;q=Ibn+Sina+Persian+polymath&amp;pg=PA18" rel="nofollow">18</a>. "The Persian polymath Ibn Sina (981–1037) consolidated all of this learning, along with Ancient Greek and Indian knowledge, into his The Canon of Medicine (1025), a work still taught in European medical schools in the seventeenth century."</li></ul>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFAdamson2016">Adamson 2016</a>, pp. 113, 117, 206.<br/>(page 113) "For one thing, it means that he[Avicenna] had a Persian cultural background...he spoke Persian natively and did use it to write philosophy."<br/>(page 117) "But for the time being, it was a Persian from Khurasan who would have commentaries lavished upon him. Avicenna would be known by the honorific of "leading master" (al-shaykh al-raʾis)."<br/>(page 206) "Persians like Avicenna"</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFBennison2009">Bennison 2009</a>, p. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/greatcaliphsgold00benn/page/n205" rel="nofollow">195</a>. "Avicenna was a Persian whose father served the Samanids of Khurasan and Transoxania as the administrator of a rural district outside Bukhara."</li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFPaul_Strathern2005">Paul Strathern (2005). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=0rGwOkqIqKkC&amp;q=Avicenna's+ethnicity&amp;pg=PA58" rel="nofollow"><i>A brief history of medicine: from Hippocrates to gene therapy</i></a>. <li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFRoudgari2018">Roudgari, Hassan (28 December 2018). <a class="external text" href="http://www.jimc.ir/article_81757.html" rel="nofollow">"Ibn Sina or Abu Ali Sina (ابن سینا c. 980 –1037) is often known by his Latin name of Avicenna (ævɪˈsɛnə/)"</a>. <i>Journal of Iranian Medical Council</i>. <b>1</b> (2). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISSN_(identifier)" title="ISSN (identifier)">ISSN</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/2645-338X" rel="nofollow">2645-338X</a>. <q>Avicenna was a Persian polymath and one of the most famous physicians from the Islamic Golden Age. He is known as the father of early modern medicine and his most famous work in Medicine called "The Book of Healing", which became a standard medical textbook at many European universities and remained in use up to the recent centuries.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+Iranian+Medical+Council&amp;rft.atitle=Ibn+Sina+or+Abu+Ali+Sina+%28%D8%A7%D8%A8%D9%86+%D8%B3%DB%8C%D9%86%D8%A7+c.+980+%E2%80%931037%29+is+often+known+by+his+Latin+name+of+Avicenna+%28%C3%A6v%C9%AA%CB%88s%C9%9Bn%C9%99%2F%29.&amp;rft.volume=1&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.date=2018-12-28&amp;rft.issn=2645-338X&amp;rft.aulast=Roudgari&amp;rft.aufirst=Hassan&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.jimc.ir%2Farticle_81757.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAvicenna"></span></span>
